## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: Chemical Kinetics
**Analysis**: The task models the temporal evolution of reactant concentration, which is the fundamental problem in chemical kinetics. The rate of change (dA_dt) typically depends on the current concentration (A) via power laws representing reaction order, and may include explicit time (t) dependence to account for catalyst deactivation, diffusion transients, or autocatalytic feedback.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Zero-order rate law: dA_dt = -c0 (rate independent of concentration, typical for surface-saturated catalysis or photolysis)
- First-order rate law: dA_dt = -c0 * A (rate proportional to reactant concentration, common in unimolecular steps or radioactive decay)
- Second-order rate law: dA_dt = -c0 * A**2 (bimolecular collision between identical reactants)
- General nth-order rate law: dA_dt = -c0 * A**c1 (empirical reaction order fitting for complex mechanisms)
- Catalyst deactivation kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 * A * exp(-c1 * t) (time-dependent rate constant due to active site loss or poisoning)
- Induction period kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 * A * (1 - exp(-c1 * t)) (lag phase modeling precursor activation or radical buildup)
- Diffusion-controlled transient: dA_dt = -c0 * A / (1 + c1 * t) (time-dependent rate coefficient from relaxation of the diffusion boundary layer)
- Autocatalytic reaction: dA_dt = c0 * A * (c1 - A) (product accelerates its own formation, yielding sigmoidal concentration profiles)
- Product inhibition kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 * A / (1 + c1 * A) (reaction rate retardation due to accumulated product blocking active sites)
- Aging/wear-out kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 * A * t**c1 (algebraic rate decay over time, common in material degradation or chain termination)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Reaction order identification → Power dependence on concentration (dA_dt ∝ A**n), where the exponent reveals elementary vs composite mechanistic steps
- Catalyst deactivation/poisoning → Multiplicative time decay factor on the rate coefficient (k(t) ∝ exp(-c*t) or 1/(1+c*t)), representing progressive loss of active sites
- Autocatalytic feedback → Bilinear concentration term (dA_dt ∝ A*(C-A)), indicating product-mediated acceleration that creates an initial lag followed by rapid consumption
- Diffusion-limited transient → Inverse time scaling in the rate coefficient (dA_dt ∝ A * t**(-1) or t**(-0.5)), reflecting the expansion of the concentration boundary layer
- Induction/lag phase → Time-activated exponential multiplier (dA_dt ∝ A * (1 - exp(-c*t))), capturing the delay before steady-state radical or intermediate concentrations are reached
- Reversible approach to equilibrium → Difference of forward and reverse linear terms (dA_dt = -c1*A + c2), driving the net rate asymptotically to zero as thermodynamic balance is achieved
- Photocatalytic surface recombination → Sublinear concentration dependence (dA_dt ∝ A**0.5), arising from competing adsorption-desorption and surface radical recombination processes
- Consecutive/parallel pathway competition → Superposition of multiple exponential or power-law time terms, representing the net flux from competing reaction channels

**Ground Truth (GT):**

**Candidate 5:**
`sqrt(A)*c0 + c1*cos(A*c2 + c3)`
Has `sqrt(A)`, but the second term is `cos`, not `sin`. `cos(x)` can be `sin(x + pi/2)`, but the argument is `A*c2 + c3`. Can `cos(A*c2 + c3)` match `sin(c*A)`? Yes, `cos(X) = sin(X + pi/2)`. So `c1*cos(A*c2 + c3) = c1*sin(A*c2 + c3 + pi/2)`. This is of the form `K * sin(K' * A + phase)`. The GT is `K2 * sin(K3 * A)`. A phase shift inside the sine can be absorbed if we allow constants, but wait: `sin(c*A + phi)` is not generally equal to `sin(c'*A)` unless `phi=0` or we adjust constants. Actually, `sin(c*A + phi) = sin(c*A)cos(phi) + cos(c*A)sin(phi)`. This introduces a `cos(c*A)` term. The GT only has `sin(c2*A)`. So `cos(A*c2 + c3)` cannot exactly match `sin(c*A)` for all A unless the coefficient of the cosine part is zero, which means `sin(phi)=0`, so `phi = n*pi`. Then `cos(A*c2 + n*pi) = (-1)^n cos(A*c2)`, which is still cosine, not sine. Wait, `cos(x) = sin(x + pi/2)`. So `cos(A*c2 + c3) = sin(A*c2 + c3 + pi/2)`. This is a sine function with a phase shift. The GT has `sin(c2*A)` with no phase shift. Can we match `sin(c*A + phi)` to `sin(c'*A)`? Only if `phi = 0` (or multiple of `2pi`), but then it's `sin(c*A)`. If `phi = pi/2`, it's `cos(c*A)`. So `cos` cannot match `sin` exactly without a phase shift, and the GT has no phase shift. However, sometimes symbolic regression outputs `cos` but it can be tuned to `sin` if the constant allows? No, `cos(x)` and `sin(x)` are linearly independent. So Candidate 5 is a NO MATCH.

So `cos(A*c2 + c3) = sin(A*c2 + c3 + pi/2)`. This is `sin(c2*A + phase)`. The GT has `sin(c2*A)` with no phase. Can we match `sin(c*A + phi)` to `sin(c'*A)`? ### Candidate 5:
`√A·c0 + c1·cos(A·c2 + c3)`

So this becomes: c0·√A + c1·sin(A·c2). Set c0 = -0.10017, c1 = 0.47149, c2 = 0.85551. **Perfect match!**
Candidate 5 verdict: [MATCH]

### Candidate 6:
